<a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States"><img alt="President of the United States" data-file-height="2425" data-file-width="2424" height="45" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/45px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/68px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg/90px-Seal_of_the_President_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" width="45"/></a></td>

<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;text-align:center;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Donald_Trump_series" title="Template:Donald Trump series"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Donald_Trump_series" title="Template talk:Donald Trump series"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Donald_Trump_series&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<p>The <b>2016 Republican Party presidential primaries and caucuses</b> were a series of electoral contests taking place within all 50 <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a>, and five <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">U.S. territories</a>, occurring between February 1 and June 7. Sanctioned by the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>, these elections are designed to select the 2,472 delegates to send to the <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">Republican National Convention</a>, who selected the Republican Party's <a href="/wiki/Presidential_nominee" title="Presidential nominee">nominee</a> for <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">2016 election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>. The delegates also approved the party platform and vice-presidential nominee.</p>
<p>A total of 17 major candidates entered the race starting March 23, 2015, when <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a> of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> was the first to formally announce his candidacy: he was followed by former Governor <a href="/wiki/Jeb_Bush" title="Jeb Bush">Jeb Bush</a> of Florida, retired neurosurgeon <a href="/wiki/Ben_Carson" title="Ben Carson">Ben Carson</a> of Maryland, Governor <a href="/wiki/Chris_Christie" title="Chris Christie">Chris Christie</a> of New Jersey, businesswoman <a href="/wiki/Carly_Fiorina" title="Carly Fiorina">Carly Fiorina</a> of California, former Governor <a href="/wiki/Jim_Gilmore" title="Jim Gilmore">Jim Gilmore</a> of Virginia, Senator <a href="/wiki/Lindsey_Graham" title="Lindsey Graham">Lindsey Graham</a> of South Carolina, former Governor <a href="/wiki/Mike_Huckabee" title="Mike Huckabee">Mike Huckabee</a> of Arkansas, outgoing Governor <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Jindal" title="Bobby Jindal">Bobby Jindal</a> of Louisiana, Governor <a href="/wiki/John_Kasich" title="John Kasich">John Kasich</a> of Ohio, former Governor <a href="/wiki/George_Pataki" title="George Pataki">George Pataki</a> of New York, Senator <a href="/wiki/Rand_Paul" title="Rand Paul">Rand Paul</a> of Kentucky, former Governor <a href="/wiki/Rick_Perry" title="Rick Perry">Rick Perry</a> of Texas, Senator <a href="/wiki/Marco_Rubio" title="Marco Rubio">Marco Rubio</a> of Florida, former Senator <a href="/wiki/Rick_Santorum" title="Rick Santorum">Rick Santorum</a> of Pennsylvania, businessman <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> of New York and Governor <a href="/wiki/Scott_Walker_(politician)" title="Scott Walker (politician)">Scott Walker</a> of Wisconsin. This was the largest presidential primary field for any political party in American history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Iowa_Republican_caucuses,_2016" title="Iowa Republican caucuses, 2016">Iowa caucuses</a> on February 1, Perry, Walker, Jindal, Graham and Pataki withdrew due to low polling numbers. Despite leading many polls in Iowa, Trump came in second to Cruz; Huckabee, Paul and Santorum performed poorly at the ballot box and bowed out. Following a sizable victory for Trump in the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Republican_primary,_2016" title="New Hampshire Republican primary, 2016">New Hampshire primary</a>, Christie, Fiorina and Gilmore abandoned the race. Bush capitulated after scoring fourth place to Trump, Rubio and Cruz in <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Republican_primary,_2016" title="South Carolina Republican primary, 2016">South Carolina</a>. On <a href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday" title="Super Tuesday">Super Tuesday</a>, March 1, 2016, Rubio won his first contest in Minnesota, Cruz won Alaska, Oklahoma and his home state of Texas, while Trump won seven states. Failing to gain traction, Carson suspended<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suspend_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-suspend-4">[a]</a></sup> his campaign a few days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> On March 15, 2016, nicknamed "Super Tuesday II", Kasich won his first contest in Ohio and Trump won five primaries including <a href="/wiki/Florida_Republican_primary,_2016" title="Florida Republican primary, 2016">Florida</a>. Rubio suspended his campaign after losing his home state,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> but he retained a large share of his delegates for the national convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubio_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubio-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>From March 16, 2016, to May 3, 2016, only three candidates remained in the race: Trump, Cruz and Kasich. Cruz won most delegates in four Western contests and in <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Republican_primary,_2016" title="Wisconsin Republican primary, 2016">Wisconsin</a>, keeping a credible path to denying Trump the nomination on first ballot with 1,237 delegates. However, Trump scored landslide victories in <a href="/wiki/New_York_Republican_primary,_2016" title="New York Republican primary, 2016">New York</a> and five North-Eastern states in April, before taking every delegate in the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Republican_primary,_2016" title="Indiana Republican primary, 2016">Indiana primary</a> of May 3. Without any further chances of forcing a contested convention, Cruz suspended his campaign<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNBC_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNBC-8">[7]</a></sup> and Trump was declared the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presumptive_nominee" title="Presumptive nominee">presumptive</a> Republican nominee by <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> chairman <a href="/wiki/Reince_Priebus" title="Reince Priebus">Reince Priebus</a> on the evening of May 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> Kasich dropped out the next day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> After winning the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Republican_primary,_2016" title="Washington Republican primary, 2016">Washington primary</a> and gaining support from unbound North Dakota delegates on May 26,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap-1237_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap-1237-11">[10]</a></sup> Trump passed the threshold of 1,237 delegates required to guarantee his nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Donald Trump chose Governor <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pence" title="Mike Pence">Mike Pence</a> of <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> to be his running mate, and they went on to defeat the Democratic ticket of <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> and running mate <a href="/wiki/Tim_Kaine" title="Tim Kaine">Tim Kaine</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">general election</a> held on November 8, 2016.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Candidates_and_results">Candidates and results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Candidates and results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_candidates,_2016" title="Republican Party presidential candidates, 2016">Republican Party presidential candidates, 2016</a> and <a href="/wiki/Results_of_the_Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016" title="Results of the Republican Party presidential primaries, 2016">Results of the Republican Party presidential primaries, 2016</a></div>
<p>17 major candidates were listed in <a href="/wiki/Nationwide_opinion_polling_for_the_Republican_Party_2016_presidential_primaries" title="Nationwide opinion polling for the Republican Party 2016 presidential primaries">major independent nationwide polls</a> and filed as candidates with the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Election_Commission" title="Federal Election Commission">Federal Election Commission</a>. (See the main article for other notable candidates.) A total of 2,472 delegates went to the 2016 Republican National Convention and the winning candidate needed a simple majority of 1,237 votes to be the Republican nominee.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Delegate_breakdown">Delegate breakdown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Delegate breakdown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of June 7, 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 56 primary contests have been conducted to choose 2,472 delegates. In 50 states and territories the delegates are allocated to candidates by popular vote either statewide or on the congressional district level and then elected according to state rules. In 6 states and territories, the first-instance popular vote did not allocate any delegates; they were elected later at local conventions and either bound to a candidate or uncommitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schedule_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-schedule-13">[12]</a></sup> Most delegates are elected as bound delegates, meaning that they must vote for a specific candidate on the first ballot at the <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">national convention</a>. Some delegates attended the convention as unbound or uncommitted delegates, meaning that they are free to vote for anyone at the first ballot. These 130 uncommitted delegates include 18 unbound RNC delegates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RNC_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-RNC-14">[b]</a></sup> and 112 delegates that have been elected or allocated as uncommitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[c]</a></sup> Uncommitted delegates can still express a preference for a candidate, although it is not binding. Among the 901 delegates elected for candidates who have later dropped out of the race, 155 are still bound to vote for their candidate on the first ballot<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[d]</a></sup> and 34 have been released<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suspend_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-suspend-4">[a]</a></sup> according to local rules of each state party. The voting obligations of those 712 delegates bound to the most recent withdrawn candidates (551 for Cruz and 161 for Kasich) have not yet been published. If no candidate is elected in the first round of voting, a progressively larger number of delegates are allowed to vote for a candidate of their choice. The voting rules on subsequent ballots are determined by individual states: most states release their delegates on the second round of voting, and only four states keep them bound on the third round and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbump_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbump-18">[14]</a></sup> Due to the convoluted election process and divergent definitions of delegate allegiance, even well-informed reporters had to make various assumptions to calculate and deliver estimated delegate tallies. Estimates that include unbound delegates are called "soft counts"; in contrast, the "hard count" includes only those delegates that are bound to vote for a specific candidate at the first ballot, irrespective of their personal preference. As the race progressed, conflicting estimates were reported by various media sources and election analysts, creating much commentary and speculation as to the potential path to nomination of leading candidates. The situation was only clarified after the last two opponents dropped out and Trump was declared the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_nominee" title="Presidential nominee">presumptive nominee</a> on May 3.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Delegate_count">Delegate count</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Delegate count">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>This table shows how many bound delegates each candidate had won before suspending their campaign;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suspend_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-suspend-4">[a]</a></sup> it does not show how many unbound delegates have pledged their support to any candidate during the primaries nor the expected result of the vote at the national convention. Although a state is considered won by a candidate if a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> of the state's delegates are bound, RNC Rule 40(b)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rule40_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-rule40-19">[15]</a></sup> requires that a candidate has to demonstrate support of a <a href="/wiki/Majority" title="Majority">majority</a> of delegates in eight states to be eligible as the nominee. Convention rules are based on delegate votes, not the popular vote. In the context of Republican primaries, the term "states" refers collectively to the fifty states, the District of Columbia and the five inhabited territories (altogether 56 delegations) as specified in RNC Rule 1(b).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rule1b_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-rule1b-20">[16]</a></sup> In the following table, states and territories where the candidates have achieved a majority of bound delegates are marked in bold. States and territories where a candidate won a majority of delegates but not a majority of bound delegates are marked in italics.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline_of_the_race">Timeline of the race</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Timeline of the race">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2012" title="United States presidential election, 2012">2012 presidential nominee</a> <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a> lost the election to incumbent president <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>. The Republican National Committee, believing that the long, drawn-out 2012 primary season had politically and personally damaged Romney, drafted plans to condense the 2016 primary season. As part of these plans, the <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">2016 Republican National Convention</a> was scheduled for the relatively early date of July 18–21, 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[31]</a></sup> the earliest since Republicans nominated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Dewey" title="Thomas Dewey">Thomas Dewey</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1948" title="United States presidential election, 1948">June 1948</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-39">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[33]</a></sup> By comparison, the <a href="/wiki/2012_Republican_National_Convention" title="2012 Republican National Convention">2012 Republican National Convention</a> was held August 27–30 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Times_Forum" title="Tampa Bay Times Forum">Tampa Bay Times Forum</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tampa" title="Tampa">Tampa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.</p>
<p>With Kasich's announcement to enter the race on July 21, 2015, the field reached 16 candidates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[34]</a></sup> officially becoming the largest presidential field in the history of the Republican Party, surpassing the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_1948" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 1948">1948 primaries</a>. With Gilmore's announcement to enter the race for a second time on July 30, 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[35]</a></sup> the field reached 17 candidates, becoming the largest presidential field in American history, surpassing the 16 candidates in the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries">Democratic Party presidential primaries</a> of both <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1972" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1972">1972</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1976" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1976">1976</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-December 2014, Bush, widely seen as a possible frontrunner for the nomination due to his relatively moderate stances, record as governor of a crucial swing state, name recognition and access to high-paying donors, was the first candidate to form a <a href="/wiki/Political_action_committee" title="Political action committee">political action committee</a> (PAC) and <a href="/wiki/Exploratory_committee" title="Exploratory committee">exploratory committee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[38]</a></sup> Many other candidates followed suit. The first candidate to declare his candidacy was Texas Senator <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a>, who was popular among grassroots conservatives due to his association with the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mascaro.2C_Lisa_and_David_Lauter_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mascaro.2C_Lisa_and_David_Lauter-46">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zezima.2C_Katie_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zezima.2C_Katie-47">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2016 candidates originated in several Republican Party tendencies, with the grassroots conservatives represented by Cruz and Carson, the <a href="/wiki/Christian_right" title="Christian right">Christian right</a> represented by Huckabee and Santorum, and the moderates, or establishment, represented by Bush and Christie, among others. In addition, some candidates were seen as appealing to both conservatives and moderates, such as Kasich, Jindal, Walker, Rubio and Paul. Two notable candidates from the previous primaries <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2012" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 2012">in 2012</a> returned for a second consecutive run in 2016: Santorum and Perry. Lastly, there were candidates with minimal to no political experience—Carson, Trump and Fiorina, who touted their lack of political experience as a positive while others criticized it as making them unqualified for the office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtonexaminer1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonexaminer1-50">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The field was noted for its diversity and was even called the most diverse presidential field in American history. This included two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latinos" title="Latinos">Latinos</a> (Cruz and Rubio), a woman (Fiorina), an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian-American" title="Indian-American">Indian-American</a> (Jindal) and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American">African-American</a> (Carson). Five were the sons of immigrants: Cruz (Cuban father), Jindal (Indian parents), Rubio (Cuban parents), Santorum (Italian father) and Trump (Scottish mother).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Widely viewed as a very open contest with no clear front-runner, potential candidates fluctuated in the polls for an extended period from late 2012 to the end of 2015. In the year prior to the election season, a total of 17 major candidates campaigned for the nomination, thus making it the single largest presidential primary field in American history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-2">[2]</a></sup> However, by the time the primary season started in early 2016, three candidates had clearly emerged ahead of the rest of the field: <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Marco_Rubio" title="Marco Rubio">Marco Rubio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Ted_Cruz" title="Ted Cruz">Ted Cruz</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> businessman <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>. Trump maintained wide poll leads throughout 2015 and into 2016, primarily due to his brash and unapologetic style of speaking and campaigning, emphasizing a disregard for <a href="/wiki/Political_correctness" title="Political correctness">political correctness</a>, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Populist" title="Populist">populist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nativism_(politics)" title="Nativism (politics)">nativist</a> policies, earning him the support of working-class voters and voters without a college education, among other demographics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jonahgoldberg_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-jonahgoldberg-55">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[49]</a></sup> However, this same brash attitude and polarizing policy stances generated numerous controversies in the media,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[50]</a></sup> and many of the other candidates sought to become the "anti-Trump" candidate by condemning his rhetoric and more radical policies. This gave rise to Senators Cruz and Rubio, who both emphasized their youth in comparison to most other candidates, as well as possible appeals to Hispanic voters, despite both being at different ends of the Republican political spectrum; Cruz was backed by his origins in the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a> and support among Evangelicals, while Rubio was seen by many as having broad appeal to both the conservative grassroots and the moderate “establishment” factions of the GOP, while also risking criticism from both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtonexaminer1_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonexaminer1-50">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[52]</a></sup> Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> governor <a href="/wiki/John_Kasich" title="John Kasich">John Kasich</a>, a moderate Republican, remained in the race for an extended period of time, despite widely being viewed as having little to no chance to win the nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite Trump's lead in most national polls, the first-in-the-nation <a href="/wiki/Iowa_caucuses" title="Iowa caucuses">Iowa caucuses</a> were won by Cruz, due to his support among grassroots conservatives. However, Trump rebounded with strong wins in <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>. On <a href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday" title="Super Tuesday">Super Tuesday</a>, Trump expanded his lead by winning seven of the eleven states, while the Cruz campaign gained new energy with victories in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, and the significant stronghold of Cruz’s home state <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>. Despite initially never winning a state, Rubio maintained significant momentum with narrow finishes in Iowa (third place), South Carolina (second place), and Nevada (second place), before finally claiming victory in <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> on Super Tuesday.</p>
<p>Between Super Tuesday and the beginning of the "winner-take-all" primaries, Cruz stayed nearly even with Trump, winning four states to Trump’s five, while Rubio won several smaller contests such as <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> In the first round of winner-take-all contests on March 15, Trump greatly expanded his lead by winning five of the six contests that day. After a significant loss to Trump in his home state of Florida, Rubio suspended<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suspend_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-suspend-4">[a]</a></sup> his campaign that same day. Meanwhile, Kasich finally gained some momentum by winning his home state of Ohio.</p>
<p>As the primary season entered the spring, the mostly-consolidated field resulted in a closing of the gap between Trump and Cruz, with Trump sweeping the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>, and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwest</a>, while Cruz performed strongly in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">West</a> and scored a surprise victory in <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>. Kasich, unable to win any other states besides Ohio, remained far behind in a distant third. After Cruz’s upset win in <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, speculation began to arise that the <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">convention</a> would be a brokered one in which the establishment would choose Kasich or someone else, since both Trump and Cruz were not viewed favorably by the establishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>As April came to a close and Trump won a resounding victory in his home state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, both Cruz and Kasich were mathematically eliminated from possibly becoming the nominee. Both men then formed an alliance to block Trump from winning the nomination, ahead of the "<a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela</a> primaries" of five Northeastern states on April 26.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theguardian1_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian1-63">[56]</a></sup> Subsequently, Trump swept all five states and greatly increased his delegate lead. In a final push to block Trump’s path to the nomination, Cruz announced that one of the former candidates for the nomination, former <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a> CEO <a href="/wiki/Carly_Fiorina" title="Carly Fiorina">Carly Fiorina</a>, would be his running mate if he was the nominee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[57]</a></sup> Nevertheless, after Trump won the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Republican_primary,_2016" title="Indiana Republican primary, 2016">Indiana primary</a> on May 3, Cruz suspended his campaign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNBC_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-CNBC-8">[7]</a></sup> subsequently leading to <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> Chairman <a href="/wiki/Reince_Priebus" title="Reince Priebus">Reince Priebus</a> announcing Trump as the presumptive nominee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[58]</a></sup> Kasich announced the suspension of his campaign the next day, leaving Trump as the only candidate left in the race. Trump then went on to win all of the remaining primaries, sweeping the remainder of the West, Midwest and the entirety of the West Coast. With his victories in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and the remaining final states on June 7, not only did Trump officially surpass the necessary number of bound delegates, but also broke the old record of 12,034,676 (2000)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hannity1908_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-hannity1908-66">[59]</a></sup> for the highest amount of popular votes ever received by the winner of the Republican presidential primaries, with over 14 million votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpapers_convention_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-greenpapers_convention-21">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div style="float: left; margin-right: 10px;">
<div class="timeline-wrapper"><map id="timeline_e8890fae51036f19b327d5f63b440a14" name="timeline_e8890fae51036f19b327d5f63b440a14">
<area alt="Rick Perry presidential campaign, 2016" coords="247,355,311,376" href="/wiki/Rick_Perry_presidential_campaign%2C_2016" shape="rect" title="Rick Perry presidential campaign, 2016"></area>
<area alt="Scott Walker presidential campaign, 2016" coords="259,334,329,355" href="/wiki/Scott_Walker_presidential_campaign%2C_2016" shape="rect" title="Scott Walker presidential campaign, 2016"></area>
<area alt="Bobby Jindal presidential campaign, 2016" coords="328,314,397,334" href="/wiki/Bobby_Jindal_presidential_campaign%2C_2016" shape="rect" title="Bobby Jindal presidential campaign, 2016"></area>

</div>

width="300"/></a></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012.E2.80.932014:_Fluctuating_polls">2012–2014: Fluctuating polls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 2012–2014: Fluctuating polls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chris_Christie_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Chris_Christie_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg/250px-Chris_Christie_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Chris_Christie_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg/375px-Chris_Christie_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Chris_Christie_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg/500px-Chris_Christie_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chris_Christie_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Governor Chris Christie polled highly until the 2014 "<a href="/wiki/Fort_Lee_lane_closure_scandal" title="Fort Lee lane closure scandal">Bridgegate</a>" scandal. He suspended his campaign after falling below the threshold in New Hampshire.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Romney's unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2012" title="United States presidential election, 2012">2012</a> campaign, the potential 2016 field was left without a clear future nominee, similar to that of <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2008" title="United States presidential election, 2008">2008</a>. Different speculations began rising from all sides of the right-leaning political spectrum as to who would make the best possible nominee: One faction of candidates included young freshmen senators, some with alliances to the <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a>, such as Cruz, Paul and Rubio, who in particular was the focus of attention immediately following 2012. In most national polls from late-2012 to mid-2013, Rubio was leading due to being young, articulate, having a broad appeal among conservatives and moderates and also for his Latino heritage and continued efforts on immigration reform, which many viewed as possible tools to draw Hispanic voters to the GOP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, another narrative for the nomination, similar to that which drove Romney's 2012 campaign, was that the nominee needed to be a governor in a traditionally Democratic or <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing</a> state, with a proven record that would stand as proof that such a governor could be president as well. The possible candidates that fit this criteria included Bush, Gilmore, Kasich, Pataki, Walker and Christie, who in particular had been rising in popularity due to his loud and blunt manner of speaking at public events, championed by some as challenging conventional political rhetoric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup> With his record as governor of New Jersey, a heavily Democratic state, factored in, Christie overtook Rubio in the polls from mid-2013 up until early 2014, when the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lee_lane_closure_scandal" title="Fort Lee lane closure scandal">"Bridgegate" scandal</a> was first revealed and started to damage Christie's reputation and poll standing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup> Although he was later cleared of personal responsibility in the subsequent investigation, Christie never regained frontrunner status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Christie's fall in the polls, the polls fluctuated from January to November 2014; candidates who often performed well included Paul, Wisconsin congressman and 2012 vice presidential nominee <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ryan" title="Paul Ryan">Paul Ryan</a> (the eventual House Speaker) and former candidates such as former Arkansas governor <a href="/wiki/Mike_Huckabee" title="Mike Huckabee">Mike Huckabee</a> and then-governor of Texas <a href="/wiki/Rick_Perry" title="Rick Perry">Rick Perry</a>, further reflecting the uncertainty of the upcoming race for the nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost1-75">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-realclearpolitics1_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-realclearpolitics1-76">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="April_2014.E2.80.93January_2015:_Jeb_Bush_leading_the_polls">April 2014–January 2015: Jeb Bush leading the polls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: April 2014–January 2015: Jeb Bush leading the polls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Governor_of_Florida_Jeb_Bush_at_VFW_in_Hudson,_New_Hampshire,_July_8th,_2015_by_Michael_Vadon_a_03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3840" data-file-width="5760" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Governor_of_Florida_Jeb_Bush_at_VFW_in_Hudson%2C_New_Hampshire%2C_July_8th%2C_2015_by_Michael_Vadon_a_03.jpg/250px-Governor_of_Florida_Jeb_Bush_at_VFW_in_Hudson%2C_New_Hampshire%2C_July_8th%2C_2015_by_Michael_Vadon_a_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Governor_of_Florida_Jeb_Bush_at_VFW_in_Hudson%2C_New_Hampshire%2C_July_8th%2C_2015_by_Michael_Vadon_a_03.jpg/375px-Governor_of_Florida_Jeb_Bush_at_VFW_in_Hudson%2C_New_Hampshire%2C_July_8th%2C_2015_by_Michael_Vadon_a_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Governor_of_Florida_Jeb_Bush_at_VFW_in_Hudson%2C_New_Hampshire%2C_July_8th%2C_2015_by_Michael_Vadon_a_03.jpg/500px-Governor_of_Florida_Jeb_Bush_at_VFW_in_Hudson%2C_New_Hampshire%2C_July_8th%2C_2015_by_Michael_Vadon_a_03.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Governor_of_Florida_Jeb_Bush_at_VFW_in_Hudson,_New_Hampshire,_July_8th,_2015_by_Michael_Vadon_a_03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Despite holding an unsteady lead in most of 2014 and early 2015, former Florida Governor Jeb Bush was unable to garner popular support and suspended<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suspend_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-suspend-4">[a]</a></sup> his campaign following the South Carolina primary.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In April 2014, Robert Costa and Philip Rucker of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> reported that the period of networking and relationship-building that they dubbed the "credentials caucus" had begun, with prospective candidates "quietly studying up on issues and cultivating ties to pundits and luminaries from previous administrations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though Bush often polled in the low double digits, he was considered a prominent candidate due to his high fundraising ability, record as governor of Florida (a crucial swing state) and apparent electability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[72]</a></sup> By November 2014, Bush had finally solidified his lead in the polls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[74]</a></sup> Around this time there were talks of the possibility of Romney making a third run for the presidency. During this period from November 2014 until late January 2015, the speculation fueled Romney's rise in many national polls as well, challenging Bush.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[75]</a></sup> Although Romney admitted he was entertaining the idea after initially declining, he ultimately reaffirmed his decision not to run on January 30, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, by the end of February, another challenger rose to match Bush in the polls: Walker, who often touted his record as governor in a traditionally Democratic state, particularly noting his victory in <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_gubernatorial_recall_election" title="Wisconsin gubernatorial recall election">a recall election</a> in 2012 (the first governor in American history to do so), combined with his <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_gubernatorial_election,_2014" title="Wisconsin gubernatorial election, 2014">reelection in 2014</a>. Walker and Bush balanced out in the polls from late February until about mid-June, at which point Trump entered the race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[77]</a></sup> Walker's challenge to Bush also allowed other candidates to briefly resurge in some polls from late April up until mid-June, including former top performers Rubio, Paul and Huckabee, in addition to several newcomers to the top tier of polling, including Cruz and Carson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost1_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost1-75">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-realclearpolitics1_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-realclearpolitics1-76">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-2015:_Donald_Trump_and_the_rise_of_the_outsiders">Mid-2015: Donald Trump and the rise of the outsiders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mid-2015: Donald Trump and the rise of the outsiders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Donald_Trump_Signs_The_Pledge_14.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5792" data-file-width="8688" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Donald_Trump_Signs_The_Pledge_14.jpg/250px-Donald_Trump_Signs_The_Pledge_14.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Donald_Trump_Signs_The_Pledge_14.jpg/375px-Donald_Trump_Signs_The_Pledge_14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Donald_Trump_Signs_The_Pledge_14.jpg/500px-Donald_Trump_Signs_The_Pledge_14.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Donald_Trump_Signs_The_Pledge_14.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Donald Trump's poll numbers surged as he entered the race and he held a strong lead entering the primary season. After losing Iowa to Cruz, Trump won the next three February primaries.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after Trump announced his candidacy on June 16, 2015, many pundits noted his uniquely outspoken nature, blunt language and <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>, often directly contradicting traditional political candidates. This style was seen as resonating strongly with potential Republican primary voters and Trump began to rise in the polls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[78]</a></sup> After a few weeks of briefly matching Bush, Trump surged into first place in all major national polls by mid-July,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambell1_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambell1-86">[79]</a></sup> which he continued to lead consistently until November. Trump also polled well in the early-voting states of Iowa, New Hampshire and South Carolina, often leading or coming in second in those states.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carly_Fiorina_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2297" data-file-width="3445" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carly_Fiorina_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg/250px-Carly_Fiorina_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carly_Fiorina_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg/375px-Carly_Fiorina_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Carly_Fiorina_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg/500px-Carly_Fiorina_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carly_Fiorina_by_Gage_Skidmore_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Although Carly Fiorina rose in the polls after the second debate, she failed to capitalize on her momentum and faded quickly. She ultimately suspended her campaign after New Hampshire. She briefly served as Cruz's running mate from April 27 to May 3, until Cruz suspended his campaign as well.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the surge of Trump, a man who had never held political office, the general focus began to shift over to other non-politician candidates, commonly known as "outsiders" and the other two outsiders in the field quickly rose in the polls as well in the wake of the first two debates: Carson, who rose into second place after a well-received performance in the first debate and Fiorina, who rose into the top three after her performance in the second debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[82]</a></sup> The rising popularity of non-politician outsiders shocked many political analysts, and fueled a new conversation about how those with no political experience or prior runs for office could appeal more to potential primary voters than career politicians and what it means for the future of the Republican party and American politics in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[85]</a></sup> In mid-September, the first two major candidates dropped out of the race. Perry suspended <a href="/wiki/Rick_Perry_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Rick Perry presidential campaign, 2016">his campaign</a> on September 11, 2015, citing his failure to qualify for the primetime debates, his subsequent failure to raise a significant amount of money and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indictment_of_Rick_Perry" title="Indictment of Rick Perry">indictment</a> as reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[87]</a></sup> Ten days later, on September 21, 2015, Walker suspended <a href="/wiki/Scott_Walker_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Scott Walker presidential campaign, 2016">his campaign</a> mainly due to his own poll numbers dropping after two lackluster debate performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_2015:_The_field_stabilizes.2C_six_candidates_gain_traction">End of 2015: The field stabilizes, six candidates gain traction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: End of 2015: The field stabilizes, six candidates gain traction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scott_Walker_by_Gage_Skidmore_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Scott_Walker_by_Gage_Skidmore_3.jpg/250px-Scott_Walker_by_Gage_Skidmore_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Scott_Walker_by_Gage_Skidmore_3.jpg/375px-Scott_Walker_by_Gage_Skidmore_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Scott_Walker_by_Gage_Skidmore_3.jpg/500px-Scott_Walker_by_Gage_Skidmore_3.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scott_Walker_by_Gage_Skidmore_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Governor Scott Walker surprised many political observers when he announced the suspension of his campaign on September 21, 2015, in Wisconsin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the end of September, most polling averages indicated that the field was finally stabilizing in terms of public opinion and six candidates in particular were gaining traction and pulling away from the rest of the field by considerable margins. Polling averages indicated the top six as Trump, Carson, Rubio, Fiorina, Bush and Cruz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpost2_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost2-96">[89]</a></sup> Trump and Carson were consistently first and second, respectively, while Fiorina was initially in third before being surpassed by Rubio and Bush and Cruz subsequently remained in fifth and sixth, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[92]</a></sup> The other candidates who had been in the top ten of polling—Christie, Huckabee, Paul and Kasich—all leveled out at roughly 3% or less, while the five remaining candidates outside the top ten—Santorum, Jindal, Pataki, Graham and Gilmore—were consistently polling below 1%. By the third debate in late October, Bush and Fiorina's numbers were also beginning to fade, while Cruz was on the rise and began coming in fourth by most poll averages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpost2_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost2-96">[89]</a></sup> The third debate only solidified these numbers: Bush and Fiorina remained in low digits as both were considered lackluster, while Cruz was widely held as the winner and rose even further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[94]</a></sup> Throughout this period, both Trump and Carson had pulled well ahead of the rest of the field and with Trump often registering in the low 30s and high 20s and Carson in the low 20s, the two of them combined often made up well over 50% of the electorate in a vast majority of national polls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpost2_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost2-96">[89]</a></sup> Later in October and in early November, Carson began to match even with Trump by most polling averages, rising into the mid 20s and often finishing either just behind or just ahead of Trump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[96]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/250px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/375px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/500px-Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ben_Carson_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An autumn surge had former neurosurgeon Ben Carson polling even with Trump at one point, but his support decreased significantly following the <a href="/wiki/November_2015_Paris_attacks" title="November 2015 Paris attacks">terrorist attacks in Paris</a>, which highlighted Carson's perceived inexperience on foreign policy. He later suspended his campaign after four last-place finishes on <a href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday" title="Super Tuesday">Super Tuesday</a> and endorsed Trump in response to Fiorina endorsing Cruz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[97]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By October, with the polls reflecting a field that seemed to be stabilizing, most commentators began to claim that the field had already established who the final four candidates—those who were in the race for the long-term and had the best chance of actually becoming the nominee—would be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Conservative_Review_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Conservative_Review-105">[98]</a></sup> The four were listed as being Trump, Carson, Rubio, and Cruz: Trump and Carson for their appeal as outsiders, as well as their opposite personalities-– with Trump's blunt nature and tough foreign policy stances, against Carson's soft-spoken nature and personal favorability—Rubio for his appeal to Hispanics and his stance on such issues as immigration reform, combined with strong debate performances and significant donor backing and Cruz for his appeal to Tea Party and Christian Conservative voters, which was seen as possibly having a strong impact in the southern states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Conservative_Review_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Conservative_Review-105">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[101]</a></sup> On November 17, 2015, Jindal became the third major Republican candidate to drop out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[102]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/November_2015_Paris_attacks" title="November 2015 Paris attacks">November 2015 Paris attacks</a>, which killed 130 people days before Jindal dropped out, were widely seen as having a significant impact on the 2016 presidential race, particularly on the Republican side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[103]</a></sup> The attacks were seen as boosting the campaigns of those with tough stances on immigration like Trump and Cruz, as well as the foreign policy hawks like Rubio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[105]</a></sup> Possibly as a result, Carson—who had previously been perceived as uninformed and relatively inarticulate on foreign policy—began to suffer in the polls, with Trump once again solidifying a double-digit lead over everyone else, while Rubio and Cruz began to steadily rise as Carson's numbers declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpost2_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost2-96">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[106]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ted_Cruz_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Ted_Cruz_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/250px-Ted_Cruz_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Ted_Cruz_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/375px-Ted_Cruz_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Ted_Cruz_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg/500px-Ted_Cruz_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ted_Cruz_by_Gage_Skidmore_7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Senator Ted Cruz saw a steady rise in the polls following the CNBC debate in late October. He began the election cycle with a win in Iowa and dropped out after being defeated by Trump in Indiana.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By December, Cruz had overtaken Carson by solidifying a base of support among Christian conservatives and averaged national polling of 18%, second only to Trump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[107]</a></sup> The non-interventionist Paul still failed to make traction at this juncture, while Carson fell down to about 10%, roughly even with Rubio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpost2_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost2-96">[89]</a></sup> On December 15, 2015, there was another presidential debate, which saw no major changes in the perceptions of the candidates. On December 21, 2015, the same day as the deadline to withdraw from the ballot in his home state of South Carolina, Graham suspended <a href="/wiki/Lindsey_Graham_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="Lindsey Graham presidential campaign, 2016">his campaign</a>. Eight days later, on December 29, Pataki, who was struggling to poll above the margin of error, suspended <a href="/wiki/George_Pataki_presidential_campaign,_2016" title="George Pataki presidential campaign, 2016">his campaign</a> as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PatakiOutUSAToday_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-PatakiOutUSAToday-115">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="January_2016:_The_road_to_the_early_primaries">January 2016: The road to the early primaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: January 2016: The road to the early primaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>2016 dawned with the several-month-long truce between Trump and Cruz being broken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[109]</a></sup> Cruz accused Trump of not being a consistent conservative or an ethical businessman, while Trump questioned the Canadian-born Cruz's constitutional eligibility to be president—candidates have to be natural-born U.S. citizens to be eligible to be president—while noting Cruz's past calls for immigration reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[111]</a></sup> This occurred as Trump and Cruz were vying for supremacy at the top of Iowa polls, in addition to both being the top two candidates in all national polls, ahead of the rest of the field by significant margins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[113]</a></sup> In the closing weeks before Iowa, Trump and Cruz ran dueling television commercials, each attacking the other's record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[114]</a></sup> Meanwhile, there was conflict between "establishment" candidates Rubio, Christie, Bush and Kasich, largely due to a media-reinforced belief that only a single establishment candidate could remain in the race past the early primaries. The establishment candidates staked their bids on strong showings in New Hampshire and both Christie and Kasich saw upticks in their polling in the weeks before the primary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[116]</a></sup> Both the Trump-Cruz conflict and the squabbling between establishment candidates was evident at the Republican debate on January 14. The Republican debate of January 28, devoid of Trump due to priorities and conflicts with moderator <a href="/wiki/Megyn_Kelly" title="Megyn Kelly">Megyn Kelly</a> after the debate in August, was the candidates' last shot at honing their message before the Iowa caucuses. Immigration and foreign policy featured prominently in this debate and many candidates used the opportunity of a "Trump-less debate" to criticize the second-place Cruz, who was also being heavily criticized by prominent Republican leaders in the weeks before Iowa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="February_2016:_Early_primaries">February 2016: Early primaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: February 2016: Early primaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Kasich_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/John_Kasich_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg/220px-John_Kasich_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/John_Kasich_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg/330px-John_Kasich_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/John_Kasich_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg/440px-John_Kasich_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Kasich_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ohio Governor John Kasich, shown here in <a href="/wiki/Nashua,_New_Hampshire" title="Nashua, New Hampshire">Nashua, New Hampshire</a>, finished second in New Hampshire after holding over 100 town hall meetings. He won his first and only state on March 15 in Ohio. He suspended his campaign on May 4.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the first-in-the-nation <a href="/wiki/Iowa_Republican_caucuses,_2016" title="Iowa Republican caucuses, 2016">Iowa caucuses</a>, Cruz won a narrow victory over Trump and Rubio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[119]</a></sup> Following poor performances in Iowa, three candidates suspended their campaigns: Huckabee—the winner of the caucuses in 2008—Santorum—the winner of the caucuses in 2012—and Paul, narrowing the field of candidates to nine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuckabeeOut_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-HuckabeeOut-127">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PaulOut_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-PaulOut-128">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup> After coming third in the Iowa caucuses, the media quickly painted Rubio as the candidate most likely to pick up the establishment mantle and ride it to the nomination following a strong performance in New Hampshire, much as McCain and Romney had done in 2008 and 2012, respectively, before both ultimately lost the general election to Obama. Rubio used this narrative to pick up a number of endorsements in the days following the Iowa caucuses. However, in the New Hampshire debate of February 6, 2016, Rubio repeated a talking point four times almost verbatim during an exchange with Christie, which led to sharp criticism of his performance in the aftermath and the beginning of the end of Rubio's campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Republican_primary,_2016" title="New Hampshire Republican primary, 2016">New Hampshire primary</a>, Trump scored a decisive victory over the rest of the field, winning the primary with 35% of the vote. Kasich, who had held over 100 town halls in the state, finished second in a surprise to many in the media. Cruz coming in third in the contest was also a shock to many, while Rubio slipped to fifth, behind Trump, Kasich, Cruz and Bush, whose campaign appeared to be revitalized following several months of apparent stagnation. After coming in seventh place in both Iowa and New Hampshire, Fiorina suspended her campaign on February 10, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[124]</a></sup> Later that same day, Christie, whose campaign was staked almost entirely on getting a strong showing in New Hampshire, suspended his campaign after coming in sixth in New Hampshire, failing to reach the minimum 10% vote threshold required to be allocated delegates from the state and qualifying for the next debate on CBS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[126]</a></sup> Later that week, Jim Gilmore, who had failed to gain traction, win delegates or be invited to most of the debates, suspended his campaign, narrowing the field to six.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>The third contest was in South Carolina. Prior to the primary, Rubio picked up the key endorsement of Governor <a href="/wiki/Nikki_Haley" title="Nikki Haley">Nikki Haley</a>, a feat seen as renewing his momentum after a disappointing finish in New Hampshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[129]</a></sup> Exit polling showed that 46% of voters had decided the week before the primary, and that the majority of these votes went to Rubio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[130]</a></sup> When the votes were counted, Trump again won by double digits, garnering 33% of the vote, ahead of Rubio with 22%, who edged out Cruz for second-place by 0.2%. Since Trump carried the vote both statewide and in each congressional district, his result netted him all 50 delegates available in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup> Following disappointing finishes in the first three contests despite outspending his competitors, Bush suspended his campaign on February 20.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three days following the South Carolina primary, Trump won the Nevada caucuses, winning with 46% of the vote with Rubio in a distant second with 24% and Cruz slightly further behind with 21%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="March_1.2C_2016:_Super_Tuesday">March 1, 2016: Super Tuesday</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: March 1, 2016: Super Tuesday">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday" title="Super Tuesday">Super Tuesday</a> voting, after the early voting in February, decided nearly half of the delegate votes needed to achieve the 1,237 votes to win the nomination at the <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">2016 Republican National Convention</a>—595 delegates at stake, to be exact. North Dakota held the last caucus on Super Tuesday, but there was no presidential straw poll and all the delegates elected later at its convention in April were unbound. Wyoming took a straw poll, but it was non-binding and no delegates were allocated on Super Tuesday. Leading up to Super Tuesday, a debate between the remaining five candidates took place in <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a> on February 25, 2016. Political rhetoric and charges heated up with Cruz and Rubio teaming up to attack Trump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[135]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Republican_Super_Tuesday_2016.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="959" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Republican_Super_Tuesday_2016.svg/310px-Republican_Super_Tuesday_2016.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Republican_Super_Tuesday_2016.svg/465px-Republican_Super_Tuesday_2016.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Republican_Super_Tuesday_2016.svg/620px-Republican_Super_Tuesday_2016.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Rubio_by_Gage_Skidmore_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Marco_Rubio_by_Gage_Skidmore_2.jpg/250px-Marco_Rubio_by_Gage_Skidmore_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Marco_Rubio_by_Gage_Skidmore_2.jpg/375px-Marco_Rubio_by_Gage_Skidmore_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Marco_Rubio_by_Gage_Skidmore_2.jpg/500px-Marco_Rubio_by_Gage_Skidmore_2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Rubio_by_Gage_Skidmore_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marco Rubio was considered a leading candidate for both establishment and tea party constituencies, polling in the top three in late 2015 and early 2016. He won his first state on Super Tuesday, capturing Minnesota. After a loss in his home state of Florida, he suspended his campaign on March 15, 2016.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Trump won the contests in Alabama, Arkansas, Georgia, Massachusetts, Tennessee, Vermont and Virginia, while Cruz netted a strong victory in his home state of Texas and added victories in Oklahoma and Alaska. Rubio won his first contest in the Minnesota Republican Caucus and finished a strong second in Virginia. Kasich won no contests, but he almost won in Vermont and finished second in Massachusetts. Carson did not win or place in any contest, netted only three delegates and though he initially expressed an intent to stay in the race, began showing signs of withdrawing in the days following Super Tuesday; he ultimately suspended his campaign on March 4, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_March_2016:_Between_Super_Tuesdays">Early March 2016: Between Super Tuesdays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Early March 2016: Between Super Tuesdays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Super_Tuesday" title="Super Tuesday">Super Tuesday</a> voting, but before winner-take-all voting was to begin, nine states, two territories and Washington, D.C. held their primaries and caucuses. During this period, 377 delegates were at stake. On March 3, 2016, the day before Carson dropped out of the race, Romney criticized Trump in <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney%27s_March_3_speech" title="Mitt Romney's March 3 speech">a heavily publicized speech</a>. Later that day, there was <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_debates_and_forums,_2016#March_3.2C_2016_.E2.80.93_Detroit.2C_Michigan" title="Republican Party presidential debates and forums, 2016">another GOP debate</a>, which again featured Trump, Cruz, Rubio and Kasich. Carson did not participate in the debate, as he announced the suspension of his campaign the next day, narrowing the field to four; he subsequently endorsed Trump on March 10, 2016, the day after Fiorina endorsed Cruz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[139]</a></sup> Meanwhile, as the prospect of a Trump nomination became more imminent, establishment Republicans pressured Romney or House Speaker <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ryan" title="Paul Ryan">Paul Ryan</a> to enter the race; Romney had already decided not to enter the race on January 30, 2015, while Ryan announced he would not enter on April 13, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 5, Cruz won the Kansas and Maine caucuses by comfortable margins. Trump narrowly won the Kentucky caucus and the Louisiana primary. Rubio and Kasich did not finish first or second on any primaries on "Super Saturday". The following day, the first of the 2016 primaries to be held in a U.S. territory went to Rubio, who won all of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>'s 23 delegates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[142]</a></sup> On March 8, Trump won in Michigan, Mississippi and Hawaii, while Cruz took the Idaho Republican Caucus, while Rubio missed delegate thresholds in Michigan, Mississippi and Idaho, finishing the night with only a single delegate. Despite some favorable polling in Michigan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[143]</a></sup> Kasich did not win any primaries, but he still picked up 17 delegates. Neither Rubio nor Kasich finished first or second in any primaries held that day.</p>
<p>In the Virgin Islands caucuses on March 10, a slate composed wholly of uncommitted delegates was initially elected. However, the entire slate was later disqualified by the territorial party and was replaced by the elected alternates-– two uncommitted, two for Rubio and one each for Cruz and Trump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[144]</a></sup> The dispute is now in court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[145]</a></sup> Also on March 10, there was a debate in Florida between the four surviving candidates, which was conducted in a more civil tone than prior debates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 12, the Guam caucuses endorsed eight uncommitted delegates and one unbound Cruz-supporting delegate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[147]</a></sup> Rubio won his final contest in D.C. and 9 delegates went to Cruz in the Wyoming county conventions. Rubio and Trump both earned one delegate and another was elected as uncommitted. Rubio's delegates stated that he would vote for Cruz on the second ballot if there was one.</p>
