<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="March_15.2C_2016:_Super_Tuesday_II">March 15, 2016: Super Tuesday II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: March 15, 2016: Super Tuesday II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>March 15 featured winner-take-all primaries in the delegate-rich states of Florida—Rubio's home state—and Ohio—Kasich's home state. There were also contests in Illinois, Missouri, North Carolina, and the Northern Mariana Islands, totaling 367 delegates. It was widely seen as a very important day in the presidential race because of the large number of winner-take-all delegates at stake. In the days leading up to Florida, the remaining candidates announced prominent endorsements and Trump and Rubio in particular spent millions on television advertisements assailing the other in Florida. The level of protest and violence at Trump rallies meanwhile became an object of criticism by other candidates; one such incident led to <a href="/wiki/2016_Donald_Trump_Chicago_rally_protest" title="2016 Donald Trump Chicago rally protest">the cancellation of a Trump event in Chicago</a> on March 13, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[148]</a></sup> On March 11, 2016, in an effort to stop Trump, Rubio told his supporters in Ohio to vote for Kasich, while Kasich refused to tell his Florida supporters to help Rubio, with a campaign spokesperson later quoted as saying: "We were going to win Ohio anyway even without his help, just as he's going to lose Florida to Trump without ours".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rubio-ohio_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-rubio-ohio-157">[149]</a></sup> The Northern Mariana Islands caucuses were the first contest to close on March 15, with Trump taking 73% of the vote and collecting all nine delegates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[150]</a></sup> In Florida, Trump netted the biggest prize of the night, handily defeating Rubio in his home state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup> Trump added to that wins in North Carolina, Illinois and Missouri. Soon after the announcement of his loss in Florida, Rubio suspended his campaign. Kasich got on the board for the first time, winning his home state of Ohio to stave off elimination. Kasich's victory in Ohio meant that the 2016 Republican primaries were the first since <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_1968" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 1968">1968</a> (and the first in which every state held a contest) in which more than three candidates won at least one state (though in <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2012" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 2012">2012</a> three candidates (<a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rick_Santorum" title="Rick Santorum">Rick Santorum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich" title="Newt Gingrich">Newt Gingrich</a>) won states and a fourth (<a href="/wiki/Ron_Paul" title="Ron Paul">Ron Paul</a>) won a territory (<a href="/wiki/Virgin_Islands" title="Virgin Islands">Virgin Islands</a>) while Democratic primaries have historically been more divided; examples are the Democratic primaries in <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1968" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1968">1968</a> (five candidates won states), <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1972" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1972">1972</a> (seven), <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1976" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1976">1976</a> (six), <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1988" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1988">1988</a> (five), <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1992" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1992">1992</a> (five), and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_2004" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 2004">2004</a> (four). The election in Missouri was very close, with Trump beating Cruz by fewer than 2,000 votes (0.21%);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[153]</a></sup> Cruz could have contested the outcome because the difference was less than 0.5%, but chose not to, thereby giving the 12 statewide delegates to Trump and 37 overall out of the 52.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[154]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_March_.E2.80.93_April_2016:_A_three-candidate_race">Late March – April 2016: A three-candidate race</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Late March – April 2016: A three-candidate race">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Donald_Trump_by_Gage_Skidmore_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Donald_Trump_by_Gage_Skidmore_5.jpg/250px-Donald_Trump_by_Gage_Skidmore_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Donald_Trump_by_Gage_Skidmore_5.jpg/375px-Donald_Trump_by_Gage_Skidmore_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Donald_Trump_by_Gage_Skidmore_5.jpg/500px-Donald_Trump_by_Gage_Skidmore_5.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Donald_Trump_by_Gage_Skidmore_5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Trump speaks at Fountain Park in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fountain_Hills" title="Fountain Hills">Fountain Hills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Seven states and one territory voted between March 22 and April 19, 2016, totaling 315 delegates with New York the largest (winner-take-most, 95), followed by Arizona (winner-take-all, 58).</p>
<p>The final debate between the candidates, which had been scheduled to take place on March 21, 2016, in <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City" title="Salt Lake City">Salt Lake City</a>, was cancelled after Trump and Kasich withdrew from the event. Trump had initially announced that he would be absent as there had been enough debates; Kasich subsequently stated that he would not attend a debate without Trump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 22, with concerns about the border, immigration and national security at the fore, Trump won all 58 delegates in Arizona. However, in Utah, Cruz garnered 69% of the vote, triggering the 50% rule to take all 40 delegates, buoyed by anti-Trump sentiment among many <a href="/wiki/Mormons" title="Mormons">Mormons</a>. All of American Samoa's delegates were uncommitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[156]</a></sup> After Trump was declared the presumptive nominee, all of the American Samoa delegates committed to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[157]</a></sup> American Samoa and Utah were the last caucuses of the 2016 Republican primary season.</p>
<p>As a Trump nomination became even more likely, the <a href="/wiki/Club_for_Growth" title="Club for Growth">Club for Growth</a> and other backers of the <a href="/wiki/Stop_Trump_movement" title="Stop Trump movement">Stop Trump movement</a> began adopting increasingly drastic strategies to derail his nomination, including all-out opposition to him in Wisconsin, seen as one opportunity to deny him the 1,237 delegates needed to clinch the nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[158]</a></sup> Trump stated that he foresaw the outbreak of riots if he were denied the nomination at the convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[159]</a></sup> Despite often being viewed as anathema to the establishment, Cruz began picking up endorsements of establishment figures dismayed by the prospect of a Trump nomination, including that of Romney just before the Utah primary, Bush on March 23, 2016, and Walker on March 29, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Toward the end of March, the competition between the three remaining candidates grew even fiercer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[161]</a></sup> The increasingly acrimonious back-and-forth between Cruz and Trump took a new turn after the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Enquirer" title="National Enquirer">National Enquirer</a></i> accused Cruz of being involved in five extramarital affairs-– claims Cruz denied, accusing Trump of planting the claim, but other sources linked it to Rubio's suspended campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[163]</a></sup> On March 29, the same day Walker endorsed Cruz, at a GOP town hall event hosted by <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a>, all three remaining Republican candidates distanced themselves from the vow they had taken in September to support their party's eventual nominee. Referring to the pledge, Kasich stated, "all of us shouldn't even have answered that question".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>April 3 had a North Dakota convention where Cruz gained support of eight delegates and Trump got one. Cruz later got six extra delegates committed to him. After Cruz dropped out, three delegates switched their support to Trump. The 13 uncommitted delegates backed Trump, giving him the support of 17 delegates and a majority. On April 5, buoyed by support from Walker and the state's talk and national radio hosts, Cruz won the statewide contests by a 48–35% margin and six congressional districts at the Wisconsin primary for 36 delegates. Trump won two <a href="/wiki/Congressional_district" title="Congressional district">congressional districts</a> for six delegates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the Wisconsin primary, the three candidates moved on to Trump's home state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> for its April 19 primary. Trump and Kasich teamed up to assail Cruz for his earlier criticism of Trump's "New York values," while Cruz reiterated his claim that Trump has an inconsistent conservative record and stated that "the only reason Kasich is attacking me now is because Kasich is afraid of going against Trump if I dropped out." Trump also received the support of former New York mayor and <a href="/wiki/Rudy_Giuliani_presidential_campaign,_2008" title="Rudy Giuliani presidential campaign, 2008">2008 presidential candidate</a> <a href="/wiki/Rudy_Giuliani" title="Rudy Giuliani">Rudy Giuliani</a> during the lead-up to the New York primary. On April 9, 2016, Cruz won the <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a> delegates after taking a solid majority, the state's four uncommitted delegates declaring support for Cruz, bringing his total delegate count in Colorado to 34. On April 16, 2016, Cruz won all 14 at-large delegates in the <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> state convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[166]</a></sup> He also received the support of RNC Committeewoman Marti Halverson, one of the 3 RNC delegates to the convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 19, Trump won New York with 59% of the vote, taking 89 of its 95 delegates. Kasich was a distant second with 25% of the vote, taking the other six delegates. Cruz was completely shut-out from receiving any New York delegates, coming in third place with only 15% of the vote, as the threshold for obtaining any delegates was 20%. Following the New York primary, Cruz was mathematically eliminated from reaching the majority of 1,237 delegates to earn the nomination, as he needed 678 more while only 674 were available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[168]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="April_26.2C_2016:_Acela_primary">April 26, 2016: Acela primary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: April 26, 2016: Acela primary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 26, 2016, five Northeastern primaries were contested—Connecticut, Delaware, Maryland, Pennsylvania and Rhode Island—and were collectively termed the "<a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a>" or "Acela primary" in reference to the <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a>,</i> which runs through the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[169]</a></sup> All five primaries were won by Trump by overwhelming margins between 29% and 41%; Trump received over 54% of the vote in all five. He claimed all the delegates available in Connecticut, Delaware and Maryland, as well as all 17 pledged delegates in Pennsylvania. Pennsylvania also elected 54 unpledged delegates and both the Trump campaign and <i>The Washington Post</i> estimated that Trump would win the support of at least 39 of the Keystone State's 54 unpledged delegates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paunpledged1_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-paunpledged1-180">[170]</a></sup> Only in Rhode Island, where delegates were allocated proportionally, did Kasich (5) or Cruz (2) win any pledged delegates. Trump attained 12 of the 19 there and 111 of the 118 by the end of the night. The night was also notable as Trump cleared 10 million votes, surpassing the vote total attained by McCain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[171]</a></sup> and Romney<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[172]</a></sup> in 2008 and 2012 respectively. The two aforementioned contests were won with the candidate receiving a majority of the popular vote nationwide in both 2008 and 2012. The next day, Trump received the support of 31 unbound delegates from Pennsylvania, while Cruz nabbed four of them, Kasich three, and five remained uncommitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[173]</a></sup> On April 28, 2016, a Trump rally in <a href="/wiki/Costa_Mesa,_California" title="Costa Mesa, California">Costa Mesa, California</a>, attracted protests that turned violent, with approximately twenty people arrested and a police car vandalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[174]</a></sup> On April 29, the Trump campaign declared that they had accumulated 1,001 delegates, only 236 from the 1,237 necessary for the nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[175]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="May_3.2C_2016:_Indiana_primary">May 3, 2016: Indiana primary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: May 3, 2016: Indiana primary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Five primaries were contested in May: Indiana, Nebraska, West Virginia, Oregon and Washington, with the Hoosier State awarding the most, with 57 delegates. By late April, Cruz and Kasich had both been eliminated from getting 1,237 delegates, but they still had a chance to accumulate enough delegates to force a contested convention in Cleveland. Realizing this, Cruz and Kasich attempted to focus their efforts in different states, with Cruz challenging Trump head-to-head in Indiana and Kasich challenging Trump in Oregon and New Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[178]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Republican_primary,_2016" title="Indiana Republican primary, 2016">Indiana primary</a>, whose delegates were awarded winner-take all statewide and by congressional district, was seen as essential to denying Trump the 1,237 delegates needed to secure the nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theguardian1_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian1-63">[56]</a></sup> Following the Acela primaries, Cruz attempted to bolster his chances by announcing that, if nominated, he would name Fiorina as his running mate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fiorinarm_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-fiorinarm-189">[179]</a></sup> (Fiorina had served as a Cruz campaign surrogate since endorsing him in March after suspending her own presidential campaign in February and Cruz hoped that Fiorina could help his campaign in Indiana and her home state of California).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fiorinarm2_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-fiorinarm2-190">[180]</a></sup> On April 29, 2016, Governor <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pence" title="Mike Pence">Mike Pence</a> of Indiana announced that he would vote for Cruz in the primary election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[181]</a></sup> Nevertheless, although Trump was outspent by a margin of more than 4-1, he handily won Indiana with 53.3% of the vote, winning a plurality in every Congressional District and taking all 57 delegates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[182]</a></sup> Cruz subsequently dropped out of the race, eliminating any hope of a contested convention in July. As a result, <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> chairman <a href="/wiki/Reince_Priebus" title="Reince Priebus">Reince Priebus</a> tweeted that Trump was the presumptive nominee in the GOP. The next day, Kasich also suspended his campaign, leaving Trump as the only candidate in the race. Many Republican leaders and even former presidential candidates endorsed Trump after the withdrawal of Kasich and Cruz, but other party leaders such as Ryan, Romney and the entire Bush family withheld their support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[183]</a></sup> although Ryan endorsed Trump on June 2, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[184]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="May_2016:_Trump_as_presumptive_nominee">May 2016: Trump as presumptive nominee</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: May 2016: Trump as presumptive nominee">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>142 delegates were awarded between the Indiana primary and the final primaries in June; however, with Trump the only candidate remaining, Washington, Oregon, West Virginia and Nebraska became essentially uncontested, although Cruz and Kasich remained on the ballot. Trump won handily in West Virginia, Nebraska and Oregon, although Kasich received one delegate from West Virginia and five in Oregon, while Cruz took five in Oregon as well. The next week, Trump won decisively in Washington State, taking 76% of the vote and 41 of 44 delegates, with the other three uncommitted.</p>

<p>On May 26, 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> announced that Trump had passed the threshold of 1,237 delegates required to guarantee his nomination,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap-1237_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-ap-1237-11">[10]</a></sup> thanks to unbound delegates from North Dakota who declared their support for Trump.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="June_2016:_Final_primaries">June 2016: Final primaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: June 2016: Final primaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Free_the_Delegates" title="Free the Delegates">Free the Delegates</a></div>
<p>The final five primary contests were held on June 7, 2016, in California, Montana, New Jersey, New Mexico and South Dakota, with California awarding the most with 172 delegates and New Jersey with 51. Though initially pegged by pundits as being an important primary, the suspension of the Cruz and Kasich campaigns following the Indiana primary made the California primary merely a formality at that point, as Trump shattered the all-time record for votes in the Republican primaries set by <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2000" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 2000">2000</a> of 12,034,676 votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hannity1908_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-hannity1908-66">[59]</a></sup> After the final primaries, his vote total was 14,015,993 (44.9%), almost 2 million votes higher than the previous record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpapers_convention_21-8"><a href="#cite_note-greenpapers_convention-21">[17]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="July_2016:_National_Convention">July 2016: National Convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: July 2016: National Convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">2016 Republican National Convention</a></div>
<p>The 2016 Republican National Convention was held from July 18–21 at the <a href="/wiki/Quicken_Loans_Arena" title="Quicken Loans Arena">Quicken Loans Arena</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>. The delegates selected the Republican presidential and vice presidential nominees and wrote the <a href="/wiki/Party_platform" title="Party platform">party platform</a>. A simple majority of 1,237 delegates was needed to win the presidential nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gop-official_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-gop-official-195">[185]</a></sup> Following Trump's decisive win in Indiana and the subsequent suspension of the Cruz and Kasich campaigns, not to mention Trump reaching the threshold following his decisive win in Washington state, it became clear that there would not be a contested convention in Cleveland. On the first ballot, Trump clinched the nomination with 1,725 delegates, 488 more than required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_finance">Campaign finance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Campaign finance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>This is an overview of the money used in the campaign as it was reported to Federal Election Committee and released on July 21, 2016. Outside groups are <a href="/wiki/Independent_expenditure" title="Independent expenditure">independent expenditure</a>-only committees, also called <a href="/wiki/Political_action_committee" title="Political action committee">PACs and SuperPACs</a>. Several such groups normally support each candidate but the numbers in the table are a total of all of them, meaning that a group of committees can be shown as <a href="/wiki/Insolvency" title="Insolvency">technically insolvent</a> even though it is not the case for all of them. The source of all the numbers is the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Responsive_Politics" title="Center for Responsive Politics">Center for Responsive Politics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[187]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Process">Process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2016&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2K16_GOPrimary.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="959" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/2K16_GOPrimary.svg/300px-2K16_GOPrimary.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/2K16_GOPrimary.svg/450px-2K16_GOPrimary.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/2K16_GOPrimary.svg/600px-2K16_GOPrimary.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2K16_GOPrimary.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Schedule of primary elections, by month and/or date</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> imposed strict new rules for states wishing to hold early contests in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_rnc_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-hill_rnc-199">[189]</a></sup> Under these rules, no state was permitted to hold a primary or caucus in January; only Iowa, New Hampshire, South Carolina and Nevada were entitled to February contests. States with primaries or caucuses in early March were to award their delegates proportionally. Any state that might have violated these rules was to have their delegation to the <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">2016 convention</a> severely cut: states with more than 30 delegates would have been deprived of all but nine, plus <abbr title="Republican National Committee">RNC</abbr> members from that state; states with fewer than 30 would have been reduced to six, plus <abbr title="Republican National Committee">RNC</abbr> members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hill_rnc_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-hill_rnc-199">[189]</a></sup> In contrast to the 2012 cycle no states violated these rules.</p>
<p>The Republican Party presidential primaries and caucuses are <a href="/wiki/Indirect_election" title="Indirect election">indirect elections</a> in which voters allocated or elect delegates to the <a href="/wiki/2016_Republican_National_Convention" title="2016 Republican National Convention">2016 Republican National Convention</a>. These delegates can be bound or unbound to vote for a particular candidate. The election of the state delegation can happen directly or indirectly as the primary election table below shows.</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Date:</b> The date given in the first column is the date of the popular primary or caucus in a particular state or territory. This event may or may not allocate delegates. Two more dates are also important in the process: the date on which Congressional District delegates are (s)elected, and the date on which state delegates are (s)elected. Some events stretch for more than one day; in these cases the date stated in the table is the final day of the event.</li>
<li><b>State delegation:</b> Each delegation is made up of up to three kinds of delegates: party members, delegates from the congressional districts and delegates from the state at-large. They can either be bound, meaning that they are legally bound to vote for a particular candidate in the first ballot at the National Convention, or they can be unbound, meaning that they are free to vote for any candidate at the National Convention. Bound delegates' voting obligations are not necessarily in line with their own personal views, and thus cannot always be used as an indicator as to how a certain delegate will vote should there be more than one ballot at the Convention. If a candidate suspends his campaign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suspend_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-suspend-4">[a]</a></sup> the delegates allocated and/or elected to him may become unbound or stay bound depending on state rules
<ul>
<li><i>RNC:</i> Three Republican National Committee members from each state will go to the National Convention as delegates. Most of them will be bound to a candidate according to the result of the primary or caucuses. Only fifteen RNC members will be unbound, their states and territories are marked with a footnote.</li>
<li><i>AL:</i> At Large delegates are elected as representatives of the whole state. Each state is allotted ten delegates and each territory six delegates. Each state can on top of this get bonus delegates based on whether it has a Republican governor, it has Republican majorities in one or all chambers of its state legislature, and whether it has Republican majorities in its delegation to the U.S. Congress, among other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenpapersGOPDelegates_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenpapersGOPDelegates-200">[190]</a></sup> With the exception of Missouri all these delegates are allocated at the state level.</li>
<li><i>CD:</i> Each Congregational District have been assigned three delegates. These are allocated either on the District or State level according to state rules.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b>Allocation:</b> Delegates can either be allocated or unallocated at the starting primary or caucuses. They can be allocated to a candidate or as uncommitted. Those delegates that are not allocated at the starting event will later be elected either bound to a candidate or uncommitted also called unbound. The contests that allocate delegates on state and district levels use the following allocating systems:
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plurality_voting_system" title="Plurality voting system">Winner-take-all</a>. The candidate receiving the most votes is allocated all the delegates. A few states allocated all their delegates based solely on the statewide vote, but most allocated the AL delegates based on the statewide vote and the CD delegates based on the district vote in each districts. This means a candidate can "win" a state without getting all the delegates in that state's delegation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">Proportional</a>. All candidates receive delegates in accordance with the popular vote at the primary or caucuses.</li>
<li>Winner-take-most. This is a hybrid of winner-take-all and proportional. The delegates are allocated proportionally but different rules ensure that only the leading candidates take part in the allocation. If a candidate gets more than a certain threshold of the votes he will be allocated all the delegates, otherwise it is allocated proportionally. High thresholds are in place in winner-take-most primaries or caucuses. Additional rules are also in place in some states.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Direct_election" title="Direct election">Direct election</a>. Instead of voting for a candidate at the primary or caucuses the voters elect the delegates directly. The delegates can state their presidential preference (and be elected on a particular candidate's "ticket") or they can be elected as uncommitted.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b>Election:</b> Delegates are elected before, at the same time or after the starting event depending on the allocation method and the state rules.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Political_convention" title="Political convention">Convention</a>. This including Congressional District Conventions, Congressional District Caucuses at State Conventions, Summer Meetings, State and <abbr title="Congressional District">CD</abbr> delegation selection meetings and State Conventions where delegates are elected. Conventions where National Delegates are not (s)elected have not been included in the tablet.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Direct_election" title="Direct election">Direct election</a>. The National Convention delegates are elected directly on the ballot. They will either be bound, as a delegation, to the result of the contest or they will individually state on the ballot what candidate they will be bound to or if they wish to be elected as unbound delegates depending.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Slate_(elections)" title="Slate (elections)">Slate</a>. A slate can be made up in three ways: all candidates submitting a slate before the primary or caucuses, the winning candidate submitting their slate after the primary or caucuses, or the State Committee or Convention making up the slates before the primary or caucuses. The allocated delegates are then selected from these slates.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)#Structure_and_composition" title="Republican Party (United States)">Committee</a>. Each state has its own rules and regulations. Ordinarily, the state GOP executive committee or its chairman selects the delegates.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>According to Rule 40(b) of the RNC Rules of the Republican Party,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rule40_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-rule40-19">[15]</a></sup> enacted prior to the 2012 convention and amended most recently in 2014, nominations at the National Convention may be made only of candidates who demonstrate the support of a majority of delegates of at least eight state delegations. Previously, this had been a lower threshold of a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> in five states. However, per Rule 42, Rules 26–42 are "Standing Rules for this convention (2012) and the temporary rules for the next convention (2016)". While the current candidates operate under these temporary rules, it is unclear whether they will remain in place for the 2016 convention.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (August 2016)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[194]</a></sup> As of March 16, 2016, RNC Chairman <a href="/wiki/Reince_Priebus" title="Reince Priebus">Reince Priebus</a> has not taken a position on the potential rule change, while others in the party advocate for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[196]</a></sup></p>

<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-suspend-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-suspend_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-suspend_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-suspend_4-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-suspend_4-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-suspend_4-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-suspend_4-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-suspend_4-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">In US elections, suspending a campaign is a legal technicality allowing candidates to keep raising funds and paying off their debts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-RNC-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-RNC_14-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RNC_14-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RNC_14-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RNC_14-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RNC_14-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RNC_14-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RNC_14-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">RNC members from Colorado, Guam, North Dakota, Samoa, Virgin Islands and Wyoming attended the National Convention as unbound</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Uncommitted delegates have been allocated or elected in Colorado (4), Guam (6), Louisiana (5), North Dakota (25), Oklahoma (3), Pennsylvania (54), Samoa (6), Virgin Islands (2), Washington (3), West Virginia (3), and Wyoming (1)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tgp-rpu_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-tgp-rpu-15">[13]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some delegates are still bound to Bush (1), Carson (7), Fiorina (1), Huckabee (1), Paul (1) and Rubio (144)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubio_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rubio-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tgp-rpu_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-tgp-rpu-15">[13]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <b>bolded</b> states and territories, the leading candidate won the support of an <b>absolute majority</b> of that state's delegation for the first ballot; according to Rule 40(b), 8 such states are needed to be eligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rule40_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-rule40-19">[15]</a></sup> In states and territories that are not bolded, the leading candidate won the support of a simple <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> of delegates.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <b>bolded</b> states and territories, the leading candidate won the support of an <b>absolute majority</b> of that state's delegation for the first ballot; according to Rule 40(b), 8 such states are needed to be eligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rule40_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-rule40-19">[15]</a></sup> In states and territories that are not bolded, the leading candidate won the support of a simple <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> of delegates.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <b>bolded</b> states and territories, the leading candidate won the support of an <b>absolute majority</b> of that state's delegation for the first ballot; according to Rule 40(b), 8 such states are needed to be eligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rule40_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-rule40-19">[15]</a></sup> In states and territories that are not bolded, the leading candidate won the support of a simple <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> of delegates.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-uncommitted-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-uncommitted_155-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">3 uncommitted delegates were allocated from Louisiana, 5 from the Virgin Islands, 8 from Guam, 1 from Wyoming</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-CO_unbound-177"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CO_unbound_177-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Four Colorado delegates, while elected as unbound, have decided to vote for Cruz at the GOP's National Convention</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-178"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-178">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Wyoming GOP elected the remaining 14 of 26 delegates at its State Convention from April 14–16.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Iowa's delegates are bound for the first ballot to the candidates of their allocation even if the candidates have suspended their campaigns. This applies to fourteen delegates allocated to Bush, Carson, Fiorina, Huckabee, Paul and Rubio. If only one candidate is put forward for nomination at the National Convention Iowa's delegates are bound to vote for that candidate providing he received votes in caucuses</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-district-208"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-district_208-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Delegates are allocated in each Congregational District based on the statewide result - Not based on the result in the District - but they are elected at the District Conventions.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-unbound-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-unbound_210-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-unbound_210-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-unbound_210-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-unbound_210-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-unbound_210-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Delegates bound to a candidate who later suspends his campaign become unbound in some states. This applies to 3 delegates from New Hampshire elected on the Bush slate and 29 delegates allocated to Rubio from Minnesota (17), Arkansas (9), New Hampshire (2) and Wyoming (1).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubio_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rubio-7">[6]</a></sup> Additionally, Ben Carson released in writing 2 delegates allocated to him from Nevada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[197]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-211"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-211">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">3.33% refers to one delegate out of 30, or <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>30</sub></span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-choice-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-choice_212-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-choice_212-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-choice_212-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-choice_212-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-choice_212-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-choice_212-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-choice_212-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-choice_212-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Delegates indicate on the ballot what candidate they wish to be bound to or if they wish to be elected as unbound.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-213"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-213">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tennessee: half of its at-large delegates will be elected by the State Executive Committee in accordance with the binding primary result</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-214">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wyoming has only one congressional district, so the 12 CD delegates are elected in the 23 counties that are paired together.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The six uncommitted candidates originally elected at the caucus were ruled ineligible and replaced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[198]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">16 delegates allocated in CD will be elected as at-large delegates at the state convention</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">With 69% of all votes Cruz triggered the 50% threshold giving him all the delegates</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">With more than half the votes in both the state contest and 23 out of 27 district contests Trump triggered the 50% threshold giving him all the delegates awarded in these contests</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-220">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">3.57% refers to one delegate out of 28, or <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>28</sub></span>.</span></li>

<dd>* <small>These candidates are constitutionally ineligible to serve as President or Vice President.</small></dd>
</dl>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
